<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998830000000000000♠</span>0.083<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.002</span> <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force"><i>g</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calc_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-calc-15">[e]</a></sup></td>
</tr>

<th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_.28H.29" title="Absolute magnitude">Absolute magnitude <i>(H)</i></a></div>
</th>
<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2999883000000000000♠</span>−1.17<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+0.06<br/>
−0.11</span></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-absmagcalc_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-absmagcalc-20">[f]</a></sup></td>

<tr>
<th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">Angular diameter</a></div>
</th>
<td>40 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milliarcsecond" title="Milliarcsecond">milli-arcsec</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertoldi_Altenhoff_et_al._2006_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertoldi_Altenhoff_et_al._2006-21">[15]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Eris</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designation</a> <b>136199 Eris</b>) is the most massive and second-largest <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteI_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteI-22">[g]</a></sup> known in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It is also the ninth-most-massive known body directly orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteI_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteI-22">[g]</a></sup> and the largest known body in the Solar System not visited by a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. It is measured to be 2,326 ± 12 kilometers (1,445.3 ± 7.5 mi) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sicardy_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-sicardy-10">[8]</a></sup> Eris is 27% more <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">massive</a> than dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, though Pluto is slightly larger by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Pluto_larger_2015_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Pluto_larger_2015-23">[16]</a></sup> Eris' mass is about 0.27% of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-070614_eris_mass_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-070614_eris_mass-24">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eris was discovered in January 2005 by a <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a>-based team led by <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a>, and its identity was verified later that year. It is a <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian object</a> (TNO) and a member of a high-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> population known as the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disk</a>. It has one known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>. As of February 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Eris_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, its distance from the Sun is 96.3 astronomical units (1.441<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> km; 8.95<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-18">[13]</a></sup> roughly three times that of Pluto. With the exception of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">comets</a>, Eris and Dysnomia are currently the second-most-distant known natural objects in the Solar System,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDyS-distances_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstDyS-distances-25">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[h]</a></sup> the farthest object being <a href="/wiki/V774104" title="V774104">V774104</a> discovered in November 2015 at 103 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hand2015-11-10_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hand2015-11-10-29">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because Eris appeared to be larger than Pluto, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> initially described it as the Solar System's <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">tenth planet</a>. This, along with the prospect of other objects of similar size being discovered in the future, motivated the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) to <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">define the term <i>planet</i></a> for the first time. Under the <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">IAU definition</a> approved on August 24, 2006, Eris is a "dwarf planet", along with objects such as Pluto, <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUPressRelease2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUPressRelease2006-30">[22]</a></sup> thereby reducing the number of known planets in the Solar System to eight, the same as before Pluto's discovery in 1930. Observations of a stellar <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> by Eris in 2010 showed that its diameter was 2,326 ± 12 kilometers (1,445.3 ± 7.5 mi), not significantly different from that of Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2010-occult_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2010-occult-31">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2010-Plutosize_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2010-Plutosize-32">[24]</a></sup> After <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> measured Pluto's diameter as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006237200000000000♠</span>2372<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>4 km</span> in July 2015, it was determined that Eris is slightly smaller in diameter than Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Measurement_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Measurement-33">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif/220px-Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif/330px-Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif/440px-Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_showing_movement_of_2003_UB313-2-.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation showing the movement of Eris on the images used to discover it. Eris is indicated by the arrow. The three frames were taken over a period of three hours.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eris was discovered by the team of <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad_Trujillo" title="Chad Trujillo">Chad Trujillo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_L._Rabinowitz" title="David L. Rabinowitz">David Rabinowitz</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup> on January 5, 2005, from images taken on October 21, 2003. The discovery was announced on July 29, 2005, the same day as <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and two days after <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaughJohnson_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaughJohnson-34">[26]</a></sup> due in part to <a href="/wiki/Controversy_over_the_discovery_of_Haumea" title="Controversy over the discovery of Haumea">events that would later lead to controversy about Haumea</a>. The search team had been systematically scanning for large outer <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> bodies for several years, and had been involved in the discovery of several other large TNOs, including <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">50000 Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">90482 Orcus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">90377 Sedna</a>.</p>
<p>Routine observations were taken by the team on October 21, 2003, using the 1.2 m <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Oschin_telescope" title="Samuel Oschin telescope">Samuel Oschin</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schmidt_telescope" title="Schmidt telescope">Schmidt telescope</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a>, California, but the image of Eris was not discovered at that point due to its very slow motion across the sky: The team's automatic image-searching software excluded all objects moving at less than 1.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a> per hour to reduce the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_positive" title="False positive">false positives</a> returned. When <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a> was discovered, it was moving at 1.75 arcsec/h, and in light of that the team reanalyzed their old data with a lower limit on the angular motion, sorting through the previously excluded images by eye. In January 2005, the re-analysis revealed Eris's slow motion against the background <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>.</p>
<p>Follow-up observations were then carried out to make a preliminary determination of Eris's <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>, which allowed the object's distance to be estimated. The team had planned to delay announcing their discoveries of the bright objects Eris and <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> until further observations and calculations were complete, but announced them both on July 29 when the discovery of another large TNO they had been tracking, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Controversy_over_the_discovery_of_Haumea" title="Controversy over the discovery of Haumea">controversially announced</a> on July 27 by a different team in Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>More observations released in October 2005 revealed that Eris has a moon, later named <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>. Observations of Dysnomia's orbit permitted scientists to determine the mass of Eris, which in June 2007 they calculated to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7022166000000000000♠</span>(1.66<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.02)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>22</sup> kg</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-13">[10]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999270000000000000♠</span>27%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>2%</span> greater than Pluto's.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="560" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg/220px-Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg/330px-Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg/440px-Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eris_Antikensammlung_Berlin_F1775.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenian</a> painting of <a href="/wiki/Eris_(mythology)" title="Eris (mythology)">Eris</a>, c. 550 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eris is named after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> goddess <a href="/wiki/Eris_(mythology)" title="Eris (mythology)">Eris</a> (Greek <i><span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἔρις</span>),</i> a personification of strife and discord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-UB-313_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-UB-313-35">[27]</a></sup> The name was assigned on September 13, 2006, following an unusually long period in which the object was known by the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_designation_in_astronomy" title="Provisional designation in astronomy">provisional designation</a> <b><span class="nowrap">2003 UB<sub>313</sub></span></b>, which was granted automatically by the IAU under their naming protocols for <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a>. The regular adjectival form of <i>Eris</i> is <i>Eridian.</i></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Xena">Xena</span></h4>
<p>Due to uncertainty over whether the object would be classified as a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a>, because different nomenclature procedures apply to these different classes of objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-2003-UB313_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-2003-UB313-36">[28]</a></sup> the decision on what to name the object had to wait until after the August 24, 2006, IAU ruling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8747_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8747-37">[29]</a></sup> As a result, for a time the object became known to the wider public as <i>Xena</i>.</p>
<p>"Xena" was an informal name used internally by the discovery team. It was inspired by the title character of the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Xena:_Warrior_Princess" title="Xena: Warrior Princess">Xena: Warrior Princess</a></i>. The discovery team had reportedly saved the nickname "Xena" for the first body they discovered that was larger than Pluto. According to Brown,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We chose it since it started with an X (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planet_X" title="Planet X">planet "X"</a>), it sounds mythological (OK, so it's TV mythology, but Pluto is named after a cartoon, right?),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[i]</a></sup> and (this part is actually true) we've been working to get more female deities out there (<i>e.g.</i> <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>). Also, at the time, the TV show was still on TV, which shows you how long we've been searching!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAS-XenaGabrielle_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAS-XenaGabrielle-40">[31]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>"We assumed [that] a real name would come out fairly quickly, [but] the process got stalled", Mike Brown said in interview,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>One reporter [Ken Chang]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[32]</a></sup> called me up from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> who happened to have been a friend of mine from college, [and] I was a little less guarded with him than I am with the normal press. He asked me, "What's the name you guys proposed?" and I said, "Well, I'm not going to tell." And he said, "Well, what do you guys call it when you're just talking amongst yourselves?"... As far as I remember this was the only time I told anybody this in the press, and then it got everywhere, which I only sorta felt bad about—I kinda like the name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGBH_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGBH-42">[33]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Choosing_an_official_name">Choosing an official name</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_dwarf_planet_Eris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of the dwarf planet Eris. This artistic representation is based on observations made at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESO" title="ESO">ESO</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Silla" title="La Silla">La Silla</a> Observatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto.27s_Twin_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto.27s_Twin-43">[34]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to science writer <a href="/wiki/Govert_Schilling" title="Govert Schilling">Govert Schilling</a>, Brown initially wanted to call the object "<a href="/wiki/Lila_(Hinduism)" title="Lila (Hinduism)">Lila</a>", after a concept in <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a> that described the cosmos as the outcome of a game played by <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>. The name was very similar to "Lilah", the name of Brown's newborn daughter. Brown was mindful of not making his name public before it had been officially accepted. He had done so with <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a> a year previously, and had been heavily criticized. However, no objection was raised to the Sedna name other than the breach of protocol, and no competing names were suggested for Sedna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPC_20040928_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPC_20040928-44">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>He listed the address of his personal web page announcing the discovery as <i>/~mbrown/planetlila</i> and in the chaos following the <a href="/wiki/Controversy_over_the_discovery_of_Haumea" title="Controversy over the discovery of Haumea">controversy over the discovery of Haumea</a>, forgot to change it. Rather than needlessly anger more of his fellow astronomers, he simply said that the webpage had been named for his daughter and dropped "Lila" from consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schilling2008_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schilling2008-45">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brown had also speculated that <i><a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a></i>, the wife of the god <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(mythology)" title="Pluto (mythology)">Pluto</a>, would be a good name for the object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup> The name had been used several times in <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechRepublic2006_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-TechRepublic2006-46">[37]</a></sup> and was popular with the public, having handily won a poll conducted by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> magazine ("Xena", despite only being a nickname, came fourth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Neill2005-NewScientist_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Neill2005-NewScientist-47">[38]</a></sup> This was not possible once the object was classified as a dwarf planet, because there is already an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> with that name, <a href="/wiki/399_Persephone" title="399 Persephone">399 Persephone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the dispute resolved, the discovery team proposed <i><a href="/wiki/Eris_(mythology)" title="Eris (mythology)">Eris</a></i> on September 6, 2006. On September 13, 2006 this name was accepted as the official name by the IAU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caltech_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-caltech-48">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU0605_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0605-49">[40]</a></sup> Brown decided that, because the object had been considered a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> for so long, it deserved a name from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> or <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>, like the other planets. The asteroids had taken the vast majority of Graeco-Roman names. <i>Eris</i>, whom Brown described as his favorite goddess, had fortunately escaped inclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGBH_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-WGBH-42">[33]</a></sup> The name in part reflects the discord in the astronomical community caused by the debate over the object's (and Pluto's) classification.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg/220px-TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg/330px-TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg/440px-TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheTransneptunians_73AU.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of trans-Neptunian objects</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eris is a <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plutoid_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-plutoid-50">[41]</a></sup> Its orbital characteristics more specifically categorize it as a <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered-disk object</a> (SDO), or a TNO that has been "scattered" from the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> into more-distant and unusual <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> following gravitational interactions with <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> was forming. Although its high orbital inclination is unusual among the known SDOs, theoretical models suggest that objects that were originally near the inner edge of the Kuiper belt were scattered into orbits with higher inclinations than objects from the outer belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GallardoBrunini_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-GallardoBrunini-51">[42]</a></sup> Inner-belt objects are expected to be generally more massive than outer-belt objects, and so astronomers expect to discover more large objects like Eris in high-inclination orbits, which have traditionally been neglected.</p>
<p>Because Eris was initially thought to be larger than <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, it was described as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenth_planet" title="Tenth planet">tenth planet</a>" by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and in media reports of its discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL-2005-126_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL-2005-126-52">[43]</a></sup> In response to the uncertainty over its status, and because of ongoing debate over whether Pluto should be classified as a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, the IAU delegated a group of astronomers to develop a sufficiently precise definition of the term <i>planet</i> to decide the issue. This was announced as the IAU's <i><a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">Definition of a Planet in the Solar System</a>,</i> adopted on August 24, 2006. At this time, both Eris and Pluto were classified as <i><a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>,</i> a category distinct from the new definition of <i>planet.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_2006_5.266_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU_2006_5.266-53">[44]</a></sup> Brown has since stated his approval of this classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt2006_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britt2006-54">[45]</a></sup> The IAU subsequently added Eris to its <a href="/wiki/List_of_minor_planets" title="List of minor planets">Minor Planet Catalogue</a>, designating it <i>(136199) Eris.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8747_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8747-37">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eris_Orbit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="644" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Eris_Orbit.svg/220px-Eris_Orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Eris_Orbit.svg/330px-Eris_Orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Eris_Orbit.svg/440px-Eris_Orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eris_Orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The orbit of Eris (blue) compared to those of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, Neptune, and Pluto (white/gray). The arcs below the ecliptic are plotted in darker colors, and the red dot is the Sun. The diagram on the left is a polar view whereas the diagrams on the right are different views from the ecliptic.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eris has an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of 558 <a href="/wiki/Julian_year_(astronomy)" title="Julian year (astronomy)">years</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-18">[13]</a></sup> Its maximum possible distance from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> (<a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a>) is 97.65 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>, and its closest (perihelion) is 37.91 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-18">[13]</a></sup> It came to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> between 1698<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie2007_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buie2007-6">[5]</a></sup> and 1699,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horizons_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-horizons-55">[46]</a></sup> to aphelion around 1977,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horizons_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-horizons-55">[46]</a></sup> and will return to perihelion around 2256<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horizons_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-horizons-55">[46]</a></sup> to 2258.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-16">[11]</a></sup> Eris and its moon are currently the second most distant known objects in the Solar System, after <a href="/wiki/V774104" title="V774104">V774104</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hand2015-11-10_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hand2015-11-10-29">[21]</a></sup> apart from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">long-period comets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-Heavens-Above_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-Heavens-Above-56">[47]</a></sup> Approximately forty known <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">TNOs</a>, most notably <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_SQ372" title="2006 SQ372">2006 SQ<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">372</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(87269)_2000_OO67" title="(87269) 2000 OO67">2000 OO<sub>67</sub></a></span> and <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, though currently closer to the Sun than Eris, have greater average orbital distances than Eris's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semimajor_axis" title="Semimajor axis">semimajor axis</a> of 67.7 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPC_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-MPC-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eris's orbit is highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, and brings Eris to within 37.9 AU of the Sun, a typical perihelion for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scattered_disk" title="Scattered disk">scattered objects</a>. This is within the orbit of Pluto, but still safe from direct interaction with Neptune (29.8–30.4 AU). Pluto, on the other hand, like other <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>, follows a less inclined and less eccentric orbit and, protected by <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a>, can cross Neptune's orbit. It is possible that Eris is in a 17:5 resonance with Neptune, though further observations will be required to check that hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orbitsimulator_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-orbitsimulator-57">[48]</a></sup> Unlike the eight planets, whose orbits all lie roughly in the same plane as the Earth's, Eris's orbit is highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclined</a>: It is tilted at an angle of about 44 <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. In about 800 years, Eris will be closer to the Sun than Pluto for some time (see the graph at the right).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eris800yearsb.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="442" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Eris800yearsb.gif/220px-Eris800yearsb.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Eris800yearsb.gif/330px-Eris800yearsb.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Eris800yearsb.gif/440px-Eris800yearsb.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eris800yearsb.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The distances of Eris and Pluto from the Sun in the next 1,000 years</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of February 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Eris_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Eris has an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of 18.7, making it bright enough to be detectable to some amateur <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a>. A 200-millimetre (7.9 in) telescope with a <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> can detect Eris under favorable conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[j]</a></sup> The reason it had not been noticed until now is its steep orbital inclination; most searches for large outer Solar System objects concentrate on the ecliptic plane, where most bodies are found.</p>
<p>Because of the high inclination of its orbit, Eris only passes through a few constellations of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">Zodiac</a>; it is now in the constellation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetus_(constellation)" title="Cetus (constellation)">Cetus</a>. It was in <a href="/wiki/Sculptor_(constellation)" title="Sculptor (constellation)">Sculptor</a> from 1876 until 1929 and <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(constellation)" title="Phoenix (constellation)">Phoenix</a> from roughly 1840 until 1875. In 2036 it will enter <a href="/wiki/Pisces_(constellation)" title="Pisces (constellation)">Pisces</a> and stay there until 2065, when it will enter <a href="/wiki/Aries_(constellation)" title="Aries (constellation)">Aries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horizons_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-horizons-55">[46]</a></sup> It will then move into the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">northern sky</a>, entering <a href="/wiki/Perseus_(constellation)" title="Perseus (constellation)">Perseus</a> in 2128 and <a href="/wiki/Camelopardalis" title="Camelopardalis">Camelopardalis</a> (where it will reach its northernmost <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>) in 2173.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size.2C_mass_and_density">Size, mass and density</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em;">
<caption><b>Size estimates</b></caption>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Year</th>
<th>Radius (diameter)</th>
<th>Source</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">2005</th>
<td>1,199 (2,397) km<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hst1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-hst1-59">[49]</a></sup></td>
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a></i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">2007</th>
<td>1,300 (2,600) km<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-60">[50]</a></sup></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">2011</th>
<td>1,163 (2,326) km<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sicardy_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-sicardy-10">[8]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_occultation" title="Asteroid occultation">Occultation</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> of Eris was measured to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006239700000000000♠</span>2397<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>100 km</span>, using images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> (HST).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hst1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-hst1-59">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertoldi2006_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertoldi2006-61">[51]</a></sup> The size of an object is determined from its <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_.28H.29" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> (H) and the <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> (the amount of light it reflects). At a distance of 97 AU, an object with a diameter of 3,000 km would have an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_size" title="Angular size">angular size</a> of 40 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minute_of_arc" title="Minute of arc">milliarcseconds</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertoldi_Altenhoff_et_al._2006_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bertoldi_Altenhoff_et_al._2006-21">[15]</a></sup> which is directly measurable with the <i>Hubble Space Telescope</i>. Although resolving such small objects is at the very limit of its capabilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[k]</a></sup> sophisticated image processing techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Deconvolution" title="Deconvolution">deconvolution</a> can be used to measure such angular sizes fairly accurately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[l]</a></sup></p>

Artistic comparison of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <strong class="selflink">Eris</strong>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> along with the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini" style="float:right"><span style="margin-right:-0.125em">[</span>
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TNO_imagemap" title="Template:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TNO_imagemap" title="Template talk:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TNO_imagemap&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
<span style="margin-left:-0.125em">]</span></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This makes Eris around the same size as Pluto, which is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006237200000000000♠</span>2372<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>4 km</span> across. It also indicates an <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">albedo</a> of 0.96, higher than that of any other large body in the Solar System except <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sicardy_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-sicardy-10">[8]</a></sup> It is speculated that the high albedo is due to the surface ices being replenished because of temperature fluctuations as Eris's eccentric orbit takes it closer and farther from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown-2003-UB313_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-2003-UB313-65">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, a series of observations of the largest trans-Neptunian objects with the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> gave an estimate of Eris's diameter of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006260000000000000♠</span>2600<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+400<br/>
−200</span></span> km</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-60">[50]</a></sup> The Spitzer and Hubble estimates overlap in the range of 2,400–2,500 km, 4–8% larger than Pluto. Astronomers now suspect that Eris's spin axis is currently pointing toward the Sun, which would make the sunlit hemisphere warmer than average and skew any <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> measurements toward higher values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beatty2010-NewScientist_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beatty2010-NewScientist-11">[9]</a></sup> So the outcome from the 2010 Chile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_occultation" title="Asteroid occultation">occultation</a> is actually more in line with the Hubble result from 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beatty2010-NewScientist_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beatty2010-NewScientist-11">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2010, Eris was the subject of one of the most distant stellar occultations yet from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beatty2010-NewScientist_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Beatty2010-NewScientist-11">[9]</a></sup> Preliminary data from this event cast doubt on previous size estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beatty2010-NewScientist_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Beatty2010-NewScientist-11">[9]</a></sup> The teams announced their final results from the occultation in October 2011, with an estimated diameter of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006232600000000000♠</span>2326<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+6<br/>
−6</span></span> km</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sicardy_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-sicardy-10">[8]</a></sup> The mass of Eris can be calculated with much greater precision. Based on the currently accepted value for Dysnomia's period—15.774 days—<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Schaller_2007_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Schaller_2007-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown-planetlila-moon_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-planetlila-moon-66">[54]</a></sup> Eris is 27 percent more massive than Pluto. If the 2011 occultation results are used, then Eris has a density of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003252000000000000♠</span>2.52<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.07 g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-densitynote_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-densitynote-14">[d]</a></sup> substantially denser than Pluto, and thus must be composed largely of rocky materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sicardy_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-sicardy-10">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Models of internal heating via <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> suggest that Eris could have an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_ocean" title="Internal ocean">internal ocean</a> of liquid water at the mantle–core boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussman2006_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussman2006-67">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2015, after nearly ten years of Eris being considered the ninth-largest object known to directly orbit the sun, close-up imagery from the <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a> mission more accurately determined <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>'s volume to be slightly larger than Eris's, rather than slightly smaller as previously thought. Eris is now the tenth-largest object known to directly orbit the sun by volume, though not by mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBC2015-Pluto-n391321_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBC2015-Pluto-n391321-68">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_and_atmosphere">Surface and atmosphere</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="514" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/220px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/330px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png/440px-2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2003_UB313_near-infrared_spectrum.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The infrared spectrum of Eris, compared to that of Pluto, shows the marked similarities between the two bodies. Arrows denote methane absorption lines.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The discovery team followed up their initial identification of Eris with <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a> observations made at the 8 m <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_North_Telescope" title="Gemini North Telescope">Gemini North Telescope</a> in Hawaii on January 25, 2005. Infrared light from the object revealed the presence of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> ice, indicating that the surface may be similar to that of Pluto, which at the time was the only <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">TNO</a> known to have surface methane, and of Neptune's moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, which also has methane on its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gemini.edu.tenth_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-gemini.edu.tenth-69">[57]</a></sup> No surface details can be resolved from Earth or its orbit with any instrument currently available.</p>
<p>Due to Eris's distant eccentric orbit, its surface temperature is estimated to vary between about 30 and 56 K (−243.2 and −217.2 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike the somewhat reddish Pluto and Triton, Eris appears almost white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Planet_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-New_Planet-2">[2]</a></sup> Pluto's reddish color is thought to be due to deposits of <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a> on its surface, and where these deposits darken the surface, the lower albedo leads to higher temperatures and the evaporation of methane deposits. In contrast, Eris is far enough from the Sun that methane can <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condense</a> onto its surface even where the albedo is low. The condensation of methane uniformly over the surface reduces any albedo contrasts and would cover up any deposits of red tholins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown-AJ2005-635_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-AJ2005-635-70">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even though Eris can be up to three times farther from the Sun than Pluto, it approaches close enough that some of the ices on the surface might warm enough to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(physics)" title="Sublimation (physics)">sublime</a>. Because methane is highly <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a>, its presence shows either that Eris has always resided in the distant reaches of the Solar System, where it is cold enough for methane ice to persist, or that the celestial body has an internal source of methane to replenish gas that escapes from its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. This contrasts with observations of another discovered TNO, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, which reveal the presence of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> ice but not methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Licandro-AA2006-458_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Licandro-AA2006-458-71">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite">Satellite</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia (moon)</a></div>
<p>In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> team at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_telescopes" title="Keck telescopes">Keck telescopes</a> in Hawaii carried out observations of the four brightest <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">TNOs</a> (Pluto, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, and Eris), using the newly commissioned <a href="/wiki/Laser_guide_star" title="Laser guide star">laser guide star</a> adaptive optics system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_Van_Dam_et_al._2006_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_Van_Dam_et_al._2006-72">[60]</a></sup> Images taken on September 10 revealed a <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moon</a> in orbit around Eris. In keeping with the "Xena" nickname already in use for Eris, Brown's team nicknamed the moon "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gabrielle_(Xena)" title="Gabrielle (Xena)">Gabrielle</a>", after the television warrior princess' sidekick. When Eris received its official name from the IAU, the moon received the name <i><a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>,</i> after the <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(deity)" title="Dysnomia (deity)">Greek goddess of lawlessness</a> who was Eris's daughter. Brown says he picked it for similarity to his wife's name, Diane. The name also retains an oblique reference to Eris's old informal name <i>Xena</i>, portrayed on TV by <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Lawless" title="Lucy Lawless">Lucy Lawless</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tytell-ST2006_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tytell-ST2006-73">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h2>
<p>It was calculated that a flyby mission to Eris could take 24.66 years using a Jupiter gravity assist, based on launch dates of 3 April 2032 or 7 April 2044. Eris would be 92.03 or 90.19 AU from the Sun when the spacecraft arrives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGranaghan_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGranaghan-74">[62]</a></sup></p>

It is only the 16th-most-massive known body in the Solar System, because <a href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size" title="List of Solar System objects by size">seven moons</a> are more massive than any known dwarf planet.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Brown is joking on this point. It was in fact <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(Disney)" title="Pluto (Disney)">the Disney character Pluto</a> that was named after the newly discovered "planet", though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venetia_Phair" title="Venetia Phair">Venetia Phair</a>, Pluto's christener, had to counter accusations her whole life that she named the planet after a cartoon dog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2007-dog-girl_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2007-dog-girl-38">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an example of an amateur image of Eris, see <a class="external text" href="http://www.moonglow.net/ccd/pictures/pc/index.html#tnos" rel="nofollow">Fred Bruenjes' Astronomy</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Resolution of the High Resolution Channel of the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">ACS</a> is 40 marcsec (milliarcseconds) and the size of 1 pixel is ~25 marcsec i.e. ~1875 km at the distance of Eris.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The reference to 'direct' measurement by the HST should not mislead into thinking that this method is as 'direct' and model-independent as measuring say <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>'s size. Basically, the method consists in finding the statistically best fit to a <i>smeared</i> image of the size of less than 2 pixels by comparing it with smeared images of the background <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, using a given computer model of the optics (<a href="/wiki/Point_spread_function" title="Point spread function">PSF</a>). A non-technical description of the method is given on <a class="external text" href="http://www.gps.caltech.edu/~mbrown/planetlila/index.html#size" rel="nofollow">Brown's page</a>, a detailed description of this approach and its limitations are discussed in a paper on <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown-AJ2004-127_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-AJ2004-127-63">[52]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>

"A Survey of Mission Opportunities to Trans-Neptunian Objects". <i>Journal of the British Interplanetary Society</i>. <b>64</b>: 296–303. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2011JBIS...64..296M" rel="nofollow">2011JBIS...64..296M</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEris+%28dwarf+planet%29&amp;rft.atitle=A+Survey+of+Mission+Opportunities+to+Trans-Neptunian+Objects&amp;rft.au=Dove%2C+G.&amp;rft.au=Emery%2C+J.+P.&amp;rft.au=Lyne%2C+J.+E.&amp;rft.au=McGranaghan%2C+R.&amp;rft.au=Sagan%2C+B.&amp;rft.au=Tullos%2C+A.&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2011JBIS...64..296M&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+the+British+Interplanetary+Society&amp;rft.pages=296-303&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=64"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
